        <a href="/wiki/G-type_main-sequence_star" title="G-type main-sequence star">
         G2V
        </a>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <th scope="row" style="padding:0.2em 0.5em 0 0.1em;line-height:1.2em;">
        <a href="/wiki/Frost_line_(astrophysics)" title="Frost line (astrophysics)">

        </a>
       </th>
       <td>
        5 AU
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mumma_5-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Mumma-5">
          <span>

          </span>

          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>

      </tr>

         </a>
        </span>

     <p>
      The
      <b>
       Solar System
      </b>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        a
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      comprises the
      <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
       Sun
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Planetary_system" title="Planetary system">
       objects that orbit it
      </a>
      , either directly or
      <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">
       indirectly
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        b
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Of those objects that orbit the Sun directly, the largest eight are the
      <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">
       planets
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        c
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      that form the
      <a href="/wiki/Planetary_system" title="Planetary system">
       planetary system
      </a>
      around it, while the remainder are significantly smaller objects, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">
       dwarf planets
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Small_Solar_System_body" title="Small Solar System body">
       small Solar System bodies
      </a>
      such as
      <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">
       comets
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">
       asteroids
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-footnoteB_13-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-footnoteB-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        d
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Of those that orbit the Sun indirectly, two are larger than the smallest planet.
     </p>
     <p>
      The Solar System
      <a href="/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System" title="Formation and evolution of the Solar System">
       formed 4.6 billion years ago
      </a>
      from the gravitational collapse of a giant interstellar
      <a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloud" title="Molecular cloud">
       molecular cloud
      </a>
      . The vast majority of the system's
      <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">
       mass
      </a>
      is in the Sun, with most of the remaining mass contained in
      <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">
       Jupiter
      </a>
      . The four smaller inner planets,
      <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">
       Mercury
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">
       Venus
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">
       Earth
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">
       Mars
      </a>
      , are
      <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">
       terrestrial planets
      </a>
      , being primarily composed of rock and metal. The four outer planets are
      <a href="/wiki/Giant_planet" title="Giant planet">
       giant planets
      </a>
      , being substantially more massive than the terrestrials. The two largest, Jupiter and
      <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">
       Saturn
      </a>
      , are
      <a href="/wiki/Gas_giant" title="Gas giant">
       gas giants
      </a>
      , being composed mainly of
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">
       hydrogen
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">
       helium
      </a>
      ; the two outermost planets,
      <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">
       Uranus
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">
       Neptune
      </a>
      , are
      <a href="/wiki/Ice_giant" title="Ice giant">
       ice giants
      </a>
      , being composed largely of substances with relatively high melting points compared with hydrogen and helium, called
      <a href="/wiki/Volatiles" title="Volatiles">
       ices
      </a>
      , such as water, ammonia and
      <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">
       methane
      </a>
      . All planets have almost circular orbits that lie within a nearly flat disc called the
      <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">
       ecliptic
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The Solar System also contains
      <a href="/wiki/Small_Solar_System_body" title="Small Solar System body">
       smaller objects
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-footnoteB_13-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-footnoteB-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        d
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">
       asteroid belt
      </a>
      , which lies between Mars and Jupiter, mostly contains objects composed, like the terrestrial planets, of rock and metal. Beyond Neptune's orbit lie the
      <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">
       Kuiper belt
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Scattered_disc" title="Scattered disc">
       scattered disc
      </a>
      , populations of
      <a href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_object" title="Trans-Neptunian object">
       trans-Neptunian objects
      </a>
      composed mostly of ices, and beyond them a newly discovered population of
      <a href="/wiki/Sednoid" title="Sednoid">
       sednoids
      </a>
      . Within these populations are several dozen to possibly tens of thousands of objects large enough to have been rounded by their own gravity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern2012_14-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Stern2012-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Such objects are categorized as
      <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">
       dwarf planets
      </a>
      . Identified dwarf planets include the asteroid
      <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">
       Ceres
      </a>
      and the trans-Neptunian objects
      <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">
       Pluto
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">
       Eris
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-footnoteB_13-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-footnoteB-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        d
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In addition to these two regions, various other small-body populations, including
      <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">
       comets
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Centaur_(minor_planet)" title="Centaur (minor planet)">
       centaurs
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interplanetary_dust" title="Interplanetary dust">
       interplanetary dust
      </a>
      , freely travel between regions. Six of the planets, at least three of the dwarf planets, and many of the smaller bodies are orbited by
      <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">
       natural satellites
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        e
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      usually termed "moons" after Earth's
      <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
       Moon
      </a>
      . Each of the outer planets is encircled by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_ring" title="Planetary ring">
       planetary rings
      </a>
      of dust and other small objects.
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">
       solar wind
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">
       plasma
      </a>
      flowing outwards from the Sun, creates a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stellar_wind_bubble" title="Stellar wind bubble">
       bubble
      </a>
      in the
      <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">
       interstellar medium
      </a>
      known as the
      <a href="/wiki/Heliosphere" title="Heliosphere">
       heliosphere
      </a>
      . The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heliopause_(astronomy)" title="Heliopause (astronomy)">
       heliopause
      </a>
      is the point at which pressure from the solar wind is equal to the opposing pressure of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstellar_wind" title="Interstellar wind">
       interstellar wind
      </a>
      ; it extends out to the edge of the
      <a href="/wiki/Scattered_disc" title="Scattered disc">
       scattered disc
      </a>
      . The
      <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">
       Oort cloud
      </a>
      , which is believed to be the source for
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long-period_comet" title="Long-period comet">
       long-period comets
      </a>
      , may also exist at a distance roughly a thousand times further than the heliosphere. The Solar System is located in the
      <a href="/wiki/Orion_Arm" title="Orion Arm">
       Orion Arm
      </a>
      , 26,000 light-years from the center of the
      <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">
       Milky Way
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

             </span>

             </span>
            </a>
           </li>
           <li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-27">

           </li>
          </ul>
         </li>

     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery_and_exploration">
       Discovery and exploration
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Heliocentric.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="570" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Heliocentric.jpg/220px-Heliocentric.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Heliocentric.jpg/330px-Heliocentric.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Heliocentric.jpg/440px-Heliocentric.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Heliocentric.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Cellarius" title="Andreas Cellarius">
         Andreas Cellarius
        </a>
        's illustration of the Copernican system, from the
        <i>
         Harmonia Macrocosmica
        </i>
        (1660)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Discovery_and_exploration_of_the_Solar_System" title="Discovery and exploration of the Solar System">
       Discovery and exploration of the Solar System
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      For many thousands of years, humanity, with a few notable exceptions, did not recognize the existence of the Solar System. People believed Earth to be stationary at the centre of the
      <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">
       universe
      </a>
      and categorically different from the divine or ethereal objects that moved through the sky. Although the
      <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">
       Greek
      </a>
      philosopher
      <a href="/wiki/Aristarchus_of_Samos" title="Aristarchus of Samos">
       Aristarchus of Samos
      </a>
      had speculated on a heliocentric reordering of the cosmos,
      <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus" title="Nicolaus Copernicus">
       Nicolaus Copernicus
      </a>
      was the first to develop a mathematically predictive
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heliocentric" title="Heliocentric">
       heliocentric
      </a>
      system.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the 17th-century,
      <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">
       Galileo Galilei
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">
       Johannes Kepler
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">
       Isaac Newton
      </a>
      , developed an understanding of
      <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">
       physics
      </a>
      that led to the gradual acceptance of the idea that Earth moves around the Sun and that the planets are governed by the same physical laws that governed Earth. The invention of the telescope led to the discovery of further planets and moons. Improvements in the telescope and the use of
      <a href="/wiki/Unmanned_spacecraft" title="Unmanned spacecraft">
       unmanned spacecraft
      </a>
      have enabled the investigation of geological phenomena, such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mountains" title="Mountains">
       mountains
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">
       craters
      </a>
      , seasonal meteorological phenomena, such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clouds" title="Clouds">
       clouds
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dust_storms" title="Dust storms">
       dust storms
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ice_caps" title="Ice caps">
       ice caps
      </a>
      on the other planets.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Structure_and_composition">
       Structure and composition
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oort_cloud_Sedna_orbit.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2499" data-file-width="2499" height="400" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Oort_cloud_Sedna_orbit.svg/400px-Oort_cloud_Sedna_orbit.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Oort_cloud_Sedna_orbit.svg/600px-Oort_cloud_Sedna_orbit.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Oort_cloud_Sedna_orbit.svg/800px-Oort_cloud_Sedna_orbit.svg.png 2x" width="400"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oort_cloud_Sedna_orbit.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">
         orbits
        </a>
        of the bodies in the Solar System to scale (clockwise from top left)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The principal component of the Solar System is the Sun, a
      <a href="/wiki/G-type_main-sequence_star" title="G-type main-sequence star">
       G2 main-sequence star
      </a>
      that contains 99.86% of the system's known mass and dominates it gravitationally.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Sun's four largest orbiting bodies, the
      <a href="/wiki/Giant_planet" title="Giant planet">
       giant planets
      </a>
      , account for 99% of the remaining mass, with Jupiter and Saturn together comprising more than 90%. The remaining objects of the Solar System (including the four
      <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">
       terrestrial planets
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">
       dwarf planets
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_moons" title="List of moons">
       moons
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">
       asteroids
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">
       comets
      </a>
      ) together comprise less than 0.002% of the Solar System's total mass.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-footnoteD_22-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-footnoteD-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        f
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Most large objects in orbit around the Sun lie near the plane of Earth's orbit, known as the
      <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">
       ecliptic
      </a>
      . The planets are very close to the ecliptic, whereas comets and
      <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">
       Kuiper belt
      </a>
      objects are frequently at significantly greater angles to it.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levison2003_23-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Levison2003-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      All the planets and most other objects orbit the Sun in the same direction that the Sun is rotating (counter-clockwise, as viewed from above Earth's north pole).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      There are
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrograde_motion" title="Retrograde motion">
       exceptions
      </a>
      , such as
      <a href="/wiki/Halley%27s_Comet" title="Halley's Comet">
       Halley's Comet
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The overall structure of the charted regions of the Solar System consists of the Sun, four relatively small inner planets surrounded by a belt of mostly rocky asteroids, and four giant planets surrounded by the Kuiper belt of mostly icy objects. Astronomers sometimes informally divide this structure into separate regions. The inner Solar System includes the four terrestrial planets and the asteroid belt. The outer Solar System is beyond the asteroids, including the four giant planets.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Since the discovery of the Kuiper belt, the outermost parts of the Solar System are considered a distinct region consisting of the objects beyond Neptune.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Most of the planets in the Solar System possess secondary systems of their own, being orbited by planetary objects called
      <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">
       natural satellites
      </a>
      , or moons (two of which are larger than the planet
      <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">
       Mercury
      </a>
      ), and, in the case of the four giant planets, by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_ring" title="Planetary ring">
       planetary rings
      </a>
      , thin bands of tiny particles that orbit them in unison. Most of the largest natural satellites are in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synchronous_rotation" title="Synchronous rotation">
       synchronous rotation
      </a>
      , with one face permanently turned toward their parent.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_laws_of_planetary_motion" title="Kepler's laws of planetary motion">
       Kepler's laws of planetary motion
      </a>
      describe the orbits of objects about the Sun. Following Kepler's laws, each object travels along an
      <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">
       ellipse
      </a>
      with the Sun at one
      <a href="/wiki/Focus_(geometry)" title="Focus (geometry)">
       focus
      </a>
      . Objects closer to the Sun (with smaller
      <a href="/wiki/Semi-major_axis" title="Semi-major axis">
       semi-major axes
      </a>
      ) travel more quickly because they are more affected by the Sun's gravity. On an elliptical orbit, a body's distance from the Sun varies over the course of its year. A body's closest approach to the Sun is called its
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion" title="Perihelion">
        perihelion
       </a>
      </i>
      , whereas its most distant point from the Sun is called its
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aphelion" title="Aphelion">
        aphelion
       </a>
      </i>
      . The orbits of the planets are nearly circular, but many comets, asteroids, and Kuiper belt objects follow highly elliptical orbits. The positions of the bodies in the Solar System can be predicted using
      <a href="/wiki/Numerical_model_of_the_Solar_System" title="Numerical model of the Solar System">
       numerical models
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;max-width:402px">
       <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:199px;max-width:199px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ecliptic_plane_3d_view.gif">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="248" data-file-width="249" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Ecliptic_plane_3d_view.gif/197px-Ecliptic_plane_3d_view.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0d/Ecliptic_plane_3d_view.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0d/Ecliptic_plane_3d_view.gif 2x" width="197"/>
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
       <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:199px;max-width:199px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solarsystem3DJupiter.gif">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="251" data-file-width="248" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Solarsystem3DJupiter.gif/197px-Solarsystem3DJupiter.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/08/Solarsystem3DJupiter.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/08/Solarsystem3DJupiter.gif 2x" width="197"/>
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
       <div style="clear:left">
       </div>
       <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">
        Solar System showing the plane of Earth's orbit around the Sun in 3D. Mercury, Venus, Earth, and Mars are shown in both panels; the right panel also shows Jupiter making one full revolution with Saturn and Uranus making less than one full revolution.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Although the Sun dominates the system by mass, it accounts for only about 2% of the
      <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum" title="Angular momentum">
       angular momentum
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marochnik1995_28-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Marochnik1995-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The planets, dominated by Jupiter, account for most of the rest of the angular momentum due to the combination of their mass, orbit, and distance from the Sun, with a possibly significant contribution from comets.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marochnik1995_28-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Marochnik1995-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Sun, which comprises nearly all the matter in the Solar System, is composed of roughly 98% hydrogen and helium.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">
       Jupiter
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">
       Saturn
      </a>
      , which comprise nearly all the remaining matter, possess atmospheres composed of roughly 99% of these elements.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A composition gradient exists in the Solar System, created by heat and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_pressure" title="Light pressure">
       light pressure
      </a>
      from the Sun; those objects closer to the Sun, which are more affected by heat and light pressure, are composed of elements with high melting points. Objects farther from the Sun are composed largely of materials with lower melting points.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The boundary in the Solar System beyond which those volatile substances could condense is known as the
      <a href="/wiki/Frost_line_(astrophysics)" title="Frost line (astrophysics)">
       frost line
      </a>
      , and it lies at roughly 5 AU from the Sun.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mumma_5-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Mumma-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The objects of the inner Solar System are composed mostly of rock,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995_34-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the collective name for compounds with high melting points, such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silicates" title="Silicates">
       silicates
      </a>
      , iron or nickel, that remained solid under almost all conditions in the
      <a href="/wiki/Protoplanetary_nebula" title="Protoplanetary nebula">
       protoplanetary nebula
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Podolak_Podolak_et_al._2000_35-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Podolak_et_al._2000-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Jupiter and Saturn are composed mainly of gases, the astronomical term for materials with extremely low melting points and high
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vapour_pressure" title="Vapour pressure">
       vapour pressure
      </a>
      , such as
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">
       hydrogen
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">
       helium
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon">
       neon
      </a>
      , which were always in the gaseous phase in the nebula.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Podolak_Podolak_et_al._2000_35-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Podolak_et_al._2000-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Ices, like
      <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">
       water
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">
       methane
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">
       ammonia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">
       hydrogen sulfide
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">
       carbon dioxide
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995_34-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      have melting points up to a few hundred kelvins.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Podolak_Podolak_et_al._2000_35-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Podolak_et_al._2000-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They can be found as ices, liquids, or gases in various places in the Solar System, whereas in the nebula they were either in the solid or gaseous phase.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Podolak_Podolak_et_al._2000_35-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Podolak_et_al._2000-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Icy substances comprise the majority of the satellites of the giant planets, as well as most of Uranus and Neptune (the so-called "
      <a href="/wiki/Ice_giant" title="Ice giant">
       ice giants
      </a>
      ") and the numerous small objects that lie beyond Neptune's orbit.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995_34-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zeilik_36-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-zeilik-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Together, gases and ices are referred to as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Volatiles" title="Volatiles">
        volatiles
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Placxo_37-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Placxo-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <table class="wikitable" style="margin:0.5em auto; width:600px;">
      <tr>
       <th>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">
          Voyager 1
         </a>
        </i>
        views the Solar System from over 6 billion km from Earth.
       </th>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td style="font-size:88%">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA00453-SolarSystem-VenusEarthJupiterSaturnUranusNeptune-Voyager1-19960913.jpg">
         <img alt="PIA00453-SolarSystem-VenusEarthJupiterSaturnUranusNeptune-Voyager1-19960913.jpg" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="620" height="484" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/PIA00453-SolarSystem-VenusEarthJupiterSaturnUranusNeptune-Voyager1-19960913.jpg/600px-PIA00453-SolarSystem-VenusEarthJupiterSaturnUranusNeptune-Voyager1-19960913.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/62/PIA00453-SolarSystem-VenusEarthJupiterSaturnUranusNeptune-Voyager1-19960913.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/62/PIA00453-SolarSystem-VenusEarthJupiterSaturnUranusNeptune-Voyager1-19960913.jpg 2x" width="600"/>
        </a>
        <center>
         <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">
          Venus
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">
          Earth
         </a>
         ("
         <a href="/wiki/Pale_Blue_Dot" title="Pale Blue Dot">
          Pale Blue Dot
         </a>
         "),
         <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">
          Jupiter
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">
          Saturn
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">
          Uranus
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">
          Neptune
         </a>
         (September 13, 1996).
        </center>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Distances_and_scales">
       Distances and scales
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Size_planets_comparison.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1181" data-file-width="2100" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Size_planets_comparison.jpg/310px-Size_planets_comparison.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Size_planets_comparison.jpg/465px-Size_planets_comparison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Size_planets_comparison.jpg/620px-Size_planets_comparison.jpg 2x" width="310"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Size_planets_comparison.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Planets of the Solar System to scale. Jupiter and Saturn (top row), Uranus and Neptune (top middle), Earth and Venus (bottom middle), Mars and Mercury.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The distance from Earth to the Sun is 1
      <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">
       astronomical unit
      </a>
      (150,000,000
      <a href="/wiki/Kilometre" title="Kilometre">
       km
      </a>
      ), or AU. For comparison, the radius of the Sun is 0.0047AU (700,000km). Thus, the Sun occupies 0.00001% (10
      <sup>
       5
      </sup>
      %) of the volume of a sphere with a radius the size of Earth's orbit, whereas Earth's volume is roughly one millionth (10
      <sup>
       6
      </sup>
      ) that of the Sun. Jupiter, the largest planet, is 5.2 astronomical units (780,000,000km) from the Sun and has a radius of 71,000km (0.00047AU), whereas the most distant planet, Neptune, is 30AU (4.5
      <span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">
       
      </span>
      10
      <sup>
       9
      </sup>
      km) from the Sun.
     </p>
     <p>
      With a few exceptions, the farther a planet or belt is from the Sun, the larger the distance between its orbit and the orbit of the next nearer object to the Sun. For example, Venus is approximately 0.33 AU farther out from the Sun than Mercury, whereas Saturn is 4.3 AU out from Jupiter, and Neptune lies 10.5 AU out from Uranus. Attempts have been made to determine a relationship between these orbital distances (for example, the
      <a href="/wiki/Titius%E2%80%93Bode_law" title="TitiusBode law">
       TitiusBode law
      </a>
      ),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but no such theory has been accepted. The images at the beginning of this section show the orbits of the various constituents of the Solar System on different scales.
     </p>
     <p>
      Some
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_System_model" title="Solar System model">
       Solar System models
      </a>
      attempt to convey the relative scales involved in the Solar System on human terms. Some are small in scale (and may be mechanicalcalled
      <a href="/wiki/Orrery" title="Orrery">
       orreries
      </a>
      )whereas others extend across cities or regional areas.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The largest such scale model, the
      <a href="/wiki/Sweden_Solar_System" title="Sweden Solar System">
       Sweden Solar System
      </a>
      , uses the 110-metre (361-ft)
      <a href="/wiki/Ericsson_Globe" title="Ericsson Globe">
       Ericsson Globe
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">
       Stockholm
      </a>
      as its substitute Sun, and, following the scale, Jupiter is a 7.5-metre (25-foot) sphere at
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arlanda_International_Airport" title="Arlanda International Airport">
       Arlanda International Airport
      </a>
      , 40km (25mi) away, whereas the farthest current object, Sedna, is a 10-cm (4-in) sphere in
      <a href="/wiki/Lule%C3%A5" title="Lule">
       Lule
      </a>
      , 912km (567mi) away.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sedna_41-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sedna-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      If the SunNeptune distance is
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_System_model" title="Solar System model">
       scaled
      </a>
      to 100 metres, then the Sun would be about 3cm in diameter (roughly two-thirds the diameter of a golf ball), the giant planets would be all smaller than about 3mm, and Earth's diameter along with the that of the other terrestrial planets would be smaller than a flea (0.3mm) at this scale.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div style="clear:both;">
     </div>
     <div class="infobox" style="float: none;">
      <div style="width:100%; margin: 0; overflow:auto; overflow-x:scroll;">
       <p>
        <map id="timeline_5fb1322f537f8a55d85170976c150191" name="timeline_5fb1322f537f8a55d85170976c150191">

       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">
