      </p>
     </div>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_and_evolution">
       Formation and evolution
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System" title="Formation and evolution of the Solar System">
       Formation and evolution of the Solar System
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solarnebula.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="318" data-file-width="430" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Solarnebula.jpg/220px-Solarnebula.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Solarnebula.jpg/330px-Solarnebula.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/28/Solarnebula.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solarnebula.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Artist's concept of the early Solar System
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The Solar System formed 4.568 billion years ago from the gravitational collapse of a region within a large
      <a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloud" title="Molecular cloud">
       molecular cloud
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This initial cloud was likely several light-years across and probably birthed several stars.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arizona_44-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Arizona-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As is typical of molecular clouds, this one consisted mostly of hydrogen, with some helium, and small amounts of heavier elements fused by previous generations of stars. As the region that would become the Solar System, known as the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_nebula" title="Solar nebula">
       pre-solar nebula
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      collapsed,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservation_of_angular_momentum" title="Conservation of angular momentum">
       conservation of angular momentum
      </a>
      caused it to rotate faster. The centre, where most of the mass collected, became increasingly hotter than the surrounding disc.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arizona_44-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Arizona-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As the contracting nebula rotated faster, it began to flatten into a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protoplanetary_disc" title="Protoplanetary disc">
       protoplanetary disc
      </a>
      with a diameter of roughly 200
      <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">
       AU
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arizona_44-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Arizona-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and a hot, dense
      <a href="/wiki/Protostar" title="Protostar">
       protostar
      </a>
      at the centre.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
       <a href="#cite_note-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The planets formed by
      <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics)" title="Accretion (astrophysics)">
       accretion
      </a>
      from this disc,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      in which dust and gas gravitationally attracted each other, coalescing to form ever larger bodies. Hundreds of protoplanets may have existed in the early Solar System, but they either merged or were destroyed, leaving the planets, dwarf planets, and leftover
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Small_solar_system_body" title="Small solar system body">
       minor bodies
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Due to their higher boiling points, only metals and silicates could exist in solid form in the warm inner Solar System close to the Sun, and these would eventually form the rocky planets of Mercury, Venus, Earth, and Mars. Because metallic elements only comprised a very small fraction of the solar nebula, the terrestrial planets could not grow very large. The giant planets (Jupiter, Saturn, Uranus, and Neptune) formed further out, beyond the frost line, the point between the orbits of Mars and Jupiter where material is cool enough for volatile icy compounds to remain solid. The ices that formed these planets were more plentiful than the metals and silicates that formed the terrestrial inner planets, allowing them to grow massive enough to capture large atmospheres of hydrogen and helium, the lightest and most abundant elements. Leftover debris that never became planets congregated in regions such as the
      <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">
       asteroid belt
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">
       Kuiper belt
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">
       Oort cloud
      </a>
      . The
      <a href="/wiki/Nice_model" title="Nice model">
       Nice model
      </a>
      is an explanation for the creation of these regions and how the outer planets could have formed in different positions and migrated to their current orbits through various gravitational interactions.
     </p>
     <p>
      Within 50 million years, the pressure and density of
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">
       hydrogen
      </a>
      in the centre of the protostar became great enough for it to begin
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">
       thermonuclear fusion
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yi2001_49-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Yi2001-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The temperature, reaction rate, pressure, and density increased until
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">
       hydrostatic equilibrium
      </a>
      was achieved: the thermal pressure equalled the force of gravity. At this point, the Sun became a
      <a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">
       main-sequence
      </a>
      star.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The main-sequence phase, from beginning to end, will last about 10 billion years for the Sun compared to around two billion years for all other phases of the Sun's pre-
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stellar_remnant" title="Stellar remnant">
       remnant
      </a>
      life combined.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mnras386_1_51-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-mnras386_1-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Solar wind from the Sun created the
      <a href="/wiki/Heliosphere" title="Heliosphere">
       heliosphere
      </a>
      and swept away the remaining gas and dust from the protoplanetary disc into interstellar space, ending the planetary formation process. The Sun is growing brighter; early in its main-sequence life its brightness was 70% that of what it is today.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Solar System will remain roughly as we know it today until the hydrogen in the core of the Sun has been entirely converted to helium, which will occur roughly 5 billion years from now. This will mark the end of the Sun's main-sequence life. At this time, the core of the Sun will collapse, and the energy output will be much greater than at present. The outer layers of the Sun will expand to roughly 260 times its current diameter, and the Sun will become a
      <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">
       red giant
      </a>
      . Because of its vastly increased surface area, the surface of the Sun will be considerably cooler (2,600 K at its coolest) than it is on the main sequence.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mnras386_1_51-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-mnras386_1-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The expanding Sun is expected to vaporize Mercury and Venus and render Earth uninhabitable as the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitable_zone" title="Habitable zone">
       habitable zone
      </a>
      moves out to the orbit of Mars. Eventually, the core will be hot enough for helium fusion; the Sun will burn helium for a fraction of the time it burned hydrogen in the core. The Sun is not massive enough to commence the fusion of heavier elements, and nuclear reactions in the core will dwindle. Its outer layers will move away into space, leaving a
      <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">
       white dwarf
      </a>
      , an extraordinarily dense object, half the original mass of the Sun but only the size of Earth.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The ejected outer layers will form what is known as a
      <a href="/wiki/Planetary_nebula" title="Planetary nebula">
       planetary nebula
      </a>
      , returning some of the material that formed the Sunbut now enriched with
      <a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">
       heavier elements
      </a>
      like carbonto the interstellar medium.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Sun">
       Sun
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
       Sun
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Planets_and_sun_size_comparison.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1181" data-file-width="2100" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Planets_and_sun_size_comparison.jpg/310px-Planets_and_sun_size_comparison.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Planets_and_sun_size_comparison.jpg/465px-Planets_and_sun_size_comparison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Planets_and_sun_size_comparison.jpg/620px-Planets_and_sun_size_comparison.jpg 2x" width="310"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Planets_and_sun_size_comparison.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The Sun compared to the planets
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The Sun is the Solar System's
      <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">
       star
      </a>
      and by far its most massive component. Its large mass (332,900 Earth masses)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      produces temperatures and densities in its
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_core" title="Solar core">
       core
      </a>
      high enough to sustain
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">
       nuclear fusion
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">
       hydrogen
      </a>
      into
      <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">
       helium
      </a>
      , making it a
      <a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">
       main-sequence
      </a>
      star.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This releases an enormous amount of
      <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">
       energy
      </a>
      , mostly
      <a href="/wiki/Radiant_energy" title="Radiant energy">
       radiated
      </a>
      into
      <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">
       space
      </a>
      as
      <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">
       electromagnetic radiation
      </a>
      peaking in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visible_light" title="Visible light">
       visible light
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Sun is a
      <a href="/wiki/G-type_main-sequence_star" title="G-type main-sequence star">
       G2-type main-sequence star
      </a>
      . Hotter main-sequence stars are more luminous. The Sun's temperature is intermediate between that of the
      <a href="/wiki/O-type_main-sequence_star" title="O-type main-sequence star">
       hottest stars
      </a>
      and that of the coolest stars. Stars brighter and hotter than the Sun are rare, whereas substantially dimmer and cooler stars, known as
      <a href="/wiki/Red_dwarf" title="Red dwarf">
       red dwarfs
      </a>
      , make up 85% of the stars in the Milky Way.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sun_57-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-sun-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Sun is a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Population_I_stars" title="Population I stars">
       population I star
      </a>
      ; it has a higher abundance of elements heavier than hydrogen and helium ("
      <a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">
       metals
      </a>
      " in astronomical parlance) than the older population II stars.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Elements heavier than hydrogen and helium were formed in the cores of ancient and exploding stars, so the first generation of stars had to die before the Universe could be enriched with these atoms. The oldest stars contain few metals, whereas stars born later have more. This high metallicity is thought to have been crucial to the Sun's development of a
      <a href="/wiki/Planetary_system" title="Planetary system">
       planetary system
      </a>
      because the planets form from the accretion of "metals".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Interplanetary_medium">
       Interplanetary medium
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Heliospheric-current-sheet.gif">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="676" data-file-width="866" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Heliospheric-current-sheet.gif/220px-Heliospheric-current-sheet.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Heliospheric-current-sheet.gif/330px-Heliospheric-current-sheet.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Heliospheric-current-sheet.gif/440px-Heliospheric-current-sheet.gif 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Heliospheric-current-sheet.gif" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Heliospheric_current_sheet" title="Heliospheric current sheet">
         heliospheric current sheet
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_medium" title="Interplanetary medium">
       Interplanetary medium
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Heliosphere" title="Heliosphere">
       Heliosphere
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">
       Solar wind
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The vast majority of the Solar System consists of a near-
      <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">
       vacuum
      </a>
      known as the
      <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_medium" title="Interplanetary medium">
       interplanetary medium
      </a>
      . Along with
      <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">
       light
      </a>
      , the Sun radiates a continuous stream of charged particles (a
      <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">
       plasma
      </a>
      ) known as the
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">
       solar wind
      </a>
      . This stream of particles spreads outwards at roughly 1.5 million km per hour,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      creating a tenuous atmosphere (the heliosphere) that permeates the interplanetary medium out to at least 100AU (see
      <a href="#Heliopause">
       heliopause
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Voyager_62-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Voyager-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Activity on the Sun's surface, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_flare" title="Solar flare">
       solar flares
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Coronal_mass_ejection" title="Coronal mass ejection">
       coronal mass ejections
      </a>
      , disturb the heliosphere, creating
      <a href="/wiki/Space_weather" title="Space weather">
       space weather
      </a>
      and causing
      <a href="/wiki/Geomagnetic_storm" title="Geomagnetic storm">
       geomagnetic storms
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SunFlip_63-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SunFlip-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The largest structure within the heliosphere is the
      <a href="/wiki/Heliospheric_current_sheet" title="Heliospheric current sheet">
       heliospheric current sheet
      </a>
      , a spiral form created by the actions of the Sun's rotating magnetic field on the interplanetary medium.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth's magnetic field">
       Earth's magnetic field
      </a>
      stops
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">
       its atmosphere
      </a>
      from being stripped away by the solar wind.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Venus and Mars do not have magnetic fields, and as a result the solar wind is causing their atmospheres to gradually bleed away into space.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Coronal_mass_ejection" title="Coronal mass ejection">
       Coronal mass ejections
      </a>
      and similar events blow a magnetic field and huge quantities of material from the surface of the Sun. The interaction of this magnetic field and material with Earth's magnetic field funnels charged particles into Earth's upper atmosphere, where its interactions create
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_(astronomy)" title="Aurora (astronomy)">
       aurorae
      </a>
      seen near the
      <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field#Magnetic_poles" title="Earth's magnetic field">
       magnetic poles
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The heliosphere and planetary magnetic fields (for those planets that have them) partially shield the Solar System from high-energy interstellar particles called
      <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">
       cosmic rays
      </a>
      . The density of cosmic rays in the
      <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">
       interstellar medium
      </a>
      and the strength of the Sun's magnetic field change on very long timescales, so the level of cosmic-ray penetration in the Solar System varies, though by how much is unknown.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Langner_et_al_2005_68-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Langner_et_al_2005-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The interplanetary medium is home to at least two disc-like regions of
      <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">
       cosmic dust
      </a>
      . The first, the
      <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_dust_cloud" title="Interplanetary dust cloud">
       zodiacal dust cloud
      </a>
      , lies in the inner Solar System and causes the
      <a href="/wiki/Zodiacal_light" title="Zodiacal light">
       zodiacal light
      </a>
      . It was likely formed by collisions within the asteroid belt brought on by gravitational interactions with the planets.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The second dust cloud extends from about 10 AU to about 40 AU, and was probably created by similar collisions within the
      <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">
       Kuiper belt
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Inner_Solar_System">
       Inner Solar System
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The inner Solar System is the traditional name for the region comprising the terrestrial planets and asteroids.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inner_72-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-inner-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Composed mainly of
      <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">
       silicates
      </a>
      and metals, the objects of the inner Solar System are relatively close to the Sun; the radius of this entire region is less than the distance between the orbits of Jupiter and Saturn. This region is also within the
      <a href="/wiki/Frost_line_(astrophysics)" title="Frost line (astrophysics)">
       frost line
      </a>
      , which is a little less than 5
      <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">
       AU
      </a>
      (about 700 million km) from the Sun.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Inner_planets">
       Inner planets
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">
       Terrestrial planet
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Telluric_planets_size_comparison.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1181" data-file-width="2100" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Telluric_planets_size_comparison.jpg/310px-Telluric_planets_size_comparison.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Telluric_planets_size_comparison.jpg/465px-Telluric_planets_size_comparison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Telluric_planets_size_comparison.jpg/620px-Telluric_planets_size_comparison.jpg 2x" width="310"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Telluric_planets_size_comparison.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The inner planets. From left to right:
        <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">
         Earth
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">
         Mars
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">
         Venus
        </a>
        , and
        <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">
         Mercury
        </a>
        (sizes to scale)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The four inner or terrestrial planets have dense,
      <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">
       rocky
      </a>
      compositions, few or no
      <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">
       moons
      </a>
      , and no
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_ring" title="Planetary ring">
       ring systems
      </a>
      . They are composed largely of
      <a href="/wiki/Refractory_(planetary_science)" title="Refractory (planetary science)">
       refractory
      </a>
      minerals, such as the
      <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">
       silicates
      </a>
      , which form their
      <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">
       crusts
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">
       mantles
      </a>
      , and metals, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">
       iron
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">
       nickel
      </a>
      , which form their
      <a href="/wiki/Planetary_core" title="Planetary core">
       cores
      </a>
      . Three of the four inner planets (Venus, Earth and Mars) have
      <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">
       atmospheres
      </a>
      substantial enough to generate
      <a href="/wiki/Weather" title="Weather">
       weather
      </a>
      ; all have
      <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">
       impact craters
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Tectonics" title="Tectonics">
       tectonic
      </a>
      surface features, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Rift_valley" title="Rift valley">
       rift valleys
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">
       volcanoes
      </a>
      . The term
      <i>
       inner planet
      </i>
      should not be confused with
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inferior_planet" title="Inferior planet">
        inferior planet
       </a>
      </i>
      , which designates those planets that are closer to the Sun than Earth is (i.e. Mercury and Venus).
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Mercury">
       Mercury
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">
       Mercury (planet)
      </a>
     </div>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">
        Mercury
       </a>
       (0.4
       <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">
        AU
       </a>
       from the Sun) is the closest planet to the Sun and the smallest planet in the Solar System (0.055 Earth masses). Mercury has no natural satellites; besides impact craters, its only known geological features are lobed ridges or
       <a href="/wiki/Rupes" title="Rupes">
        rupes
       </a>
       that were probably produced by a period of contraction early in its history.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
        <a href="#cite_note-74">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         68
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       Mercury's very tenuous atmosphere consists of atoms blasted off its surface by the solar wind.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
        <a href="#cite_note-75">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         69
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       Its relatively large iron core and thin mantle have not yet been adequately explained. Hypotheses include that its outer layers were stripped off by a giant impact; or, that it was prevented from fully accreting by the young Sun's energy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
        <a href="#cite_note-76">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         70
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
        <a href="#cite_note-77">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         71
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Venus">
       Venus
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">
       Venus
      </a>
     </div>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">
        Venus
       </a>
       (0.7 AU from the Sun) is close in size to Earth (0.815 Earth masses) and, like Earth, has a thick silicate mantle around an iron core, a substantial atmosphere, and evidence of internal geological activity. It is much drier than Earth, and its atmosphere is ninety times as dense. Venus has no natural satellites. It is the hottest planet, with surface temperatures over 400
       <a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">
        C
       </a>
       (752F), most likely due to the amount of
       <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">
        greenhouse gases
       </a>
       in the atmosphere.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
        <a href="#cite_note-78">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         72
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       No definitive evidence of current geological activity has been detected on Venus, but it has no magnetic field that would prevent depletion of its substantial atmosphere, which suggests that its atmosphere is being replenished by volcanic eruptions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
        <a href="#cite_note-79">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         73
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Earth">
       Earth
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">
       Earth
      </a>
     </div>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">
        Earth
       </a>
       (1 AU from the Sun) is the largest and densest of the inner planets, the only one known to have current geological activity, and the only place where
       <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">
        life
       </a>
       is known to exist.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-life_80-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-life-80">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         74
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       Its liquid
       <a href="/wiki/Hydrosphere" title="Hydrosphere">
        hydrosphere
       </a>
       is unique among the terrestrial planets, and it is the only planet where
       <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">
        plate tectonics
       </a>
       has been observed. Earth's atmosphere is radically different from those of the other planets, having been altered by the presence of life to contain 21% free
       <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">
        oxygen
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
        <a href="#cite_note-81">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         75
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       It has one natural satellite, the
       <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
        Moon
       </a>
       , the only large satellite of a terrestrial planet in the Solar System.
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Mars">
       Mars
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">
       Mars
      </a>
     </div>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">
        Mars
       </a>
       (1.5AU from the Sun) is smaller than Earth and Venus (0.107 Earth masses). It possesses an atmosphere of mostly
       <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">
        carbon dioxide
       </a>
       with a surface pressure of 6.1 millibars (roughly 0.6% of that of Earth).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
        <a href="#cite_note-82">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         76
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       Its surface, peppered with vast volcanoes, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Olympus_Mons" title="Olympus Mons">
        Olympus Mons
       </a>
       , and rift valleys, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Valles_Marineris" title="Valles Marineris">
        Valles Marineris
       </a>
       , shows geological activity that may have persisted until as recently as 2 million years ago.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
        <a href="#cite_note-83">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         77
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       Its red colour comes from
       <a href="/wiki/Iron(III)_oxide" title="Iron(III) oxide">
        iron oxide
       </a>
       (rust) in its soil.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
        <a href="#cite_note-84">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         78
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       Mars has two tiny natural satellites (
       <a href="/wiki/Deimos_(moon)" title="Deimos (moon)">
        Deimos
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Phobos_(moon)" title="Phobos (moon)">
        Phobos
       </a>
       ) thought to be captured
       <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">
        asteroids
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
        <a href="#cite_note-85">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         79
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Asteroid_belt">
       Asteroid belt
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">
       Asteroid belt
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:InnerSolarSystem-en.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="600" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/InnerSolarSystem-en.png/220px-InnerSolarSystem-en.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/InnerSolarSystem-en.png/330px-InnerSolarSystem-en.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/InnerSolarSystem-en.png/440px-InnerSolarSystem-en.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:InnerSolarSystem-en.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Image of the
        <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">
         asteroid belt
        </a>
        (white), the
        <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_trojan" title="Jupiter trojan">
         Jupiter trojans
        </a>
        (green), the
        <a href="/wiki/Hilda_family" title="Hilda family">
         Hildas
        </a>
        (orange), and
        <a href="/wiki/Near-Earth_object" title="Near-Earth object">
         near-Earth asteroids
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">
       Asteroids
      </a>
      are
      <a href="/wiki/Small_Solar_System_body" title="Small Solar System body">
       small Solar System bodies
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-footnoteB_13-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-footnoteB-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        d
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      composed mainly of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Refractory_(astronomy)" title="Refractory (astronomy)">
       refractory
      </a>
      rocky and metallic
      <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">
       minerals
      </a>
      , with some ice.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
       <a href="#cite_note-86">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The asteroid belt occupies the orbit between Mars and Jupiter, between 2.3 and 3.3AU from the Sun. It is thought to be remnants from the Solar System's formation that failed to coalesce because of the gravitational interference of Jupiter.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
       <a href="#cite_note-87">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        81
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Asteroids range in size from hundreds of kilometres across to microscopic. All asteroids except the largest, Ceres, are classified as small Solar System bodies.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
       <a href="#cite_note-88">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The asteroid belt contains tens of thousands, possibly millions, of objects over one kilometre in diameter.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
       <a href="#cite_note-89">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        83
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Despite this, the total mass of the asteroid belt is unlikely to be more than a thousandth of that of Earth.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krasinsky2002_21-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Krasinsky2002-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The asteroid belt is very sparsely populated;
      <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" title="Space probe">
       spacecraft
      </a>
      routinely pass through without incident. Asteroids with diameters between 10 and 10
      <sup>
       4
      </sup>
      m are called
      <a href="/wiki/Meteoroid" title="Meteoroid">
       meteoroids
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
       <a href="#cite_note-90">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        84
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Ceres">
       Ceres
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">
       Ceres (dwarf planet)
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">
       Ceres
      </a>
      (2.77AU) is the largest asteroid, a
      <a href="/wiki/Protoplanet" title="Protoplanet">
       protoplanet
      </a>
      , and a dwarf planet.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-footnoteB_13-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-footnoteB-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        d
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It has a diameter of slightly under 1,000km, and a mass large enough for its own gravity to pull it into a spherical shape. Ceres was considered a planet when it was discovered in 1801, and was reclassified to asteroid in the 1850s as further observations revealed additional asteroids.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
       <a href="#cite_note-91">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        85
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It was classified as a dwarf planet in 2006 when the
      <a href="/wiki/IAU_definition_of_planet" title="IAU definition of planet">
       definition of a planet
      </a>
      was created.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Asteroid_groups">
       Asteroid groups
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Asteroids in the asteroid belt are divided into
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asteroid_group" title="Asteroid group">
       asteroid groups
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_family" title="Asteroid family">
       families
      </a>
      based on their orbital characteristics.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asteroid_moon" title="Asteroid moon">
       Asteroid moons
      </a>
      are asteroids that orbit larger asteroids. They are not as clearly distinguished as planetary moons, sometimes being almost as large as their partners. The asteroid belt also contains
      <a href="/wiki/Main-belt_comet" title="Main-belt comet">
       main-belt comets
      </a>
      , which may have been the source of Earth's water.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
       <a href="#cite_note-92">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        86
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_trojan" title="Jupiter trojan">
       Jupiter trojans
      </a>
      are located in either of Jupiter's
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/L5_point" title="L5 point">
       L
       <sub>
        4
       </sub>
       or L
       <sub>
        5
       </sub>
       points
      </a>
      (gravitationally stable regions leading and trailing a planet in its orbit); the term "trojan" is also used for small bodies in any other planetary or satellite Lagrange point.
      <a href="/wiki/Hilda_family" title="Hilda family">
       Hilda asteroids
      </a>
      are in a 2:3
      <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">
       resonance
      </a>
      with Jupiter; that is, they go around the Sun three times for every two Jupiter orbits.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barucci_93-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Barucci-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        87
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The inner Solar System is also dusted with
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near-Earth_asteroids" title="Near-Earth asteroids">
       Near-Earth asteroids
      </a>
      , many of which cross the orbits of the inner planets.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MorbidelliAstIII_94-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-MorbidelliAstIII-94">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        88
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Outer_Solar_System">
       Outer Solar System
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The outer region of the Solar System is home to the giant planets and their large moons. The
      <a href="/wiki/Centaur_(minor_planet)" title="Centaur (minor planet)">
       centaurs
      </a>
      and many
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Short-period_comet" title="Short-period comet">
       short-period comets
      </a>
      also orbit in this region. Due to their greater distance from the Sun, the solid objects in the outer Solar System contain a higher proportion of volatiles, such as water, ammonia, and methane than those of the inner Solar System because the lower temperatures allow these compounds to remain solid.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Outer_planets">
       Outer planets
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Outer_planets" title="Outer planets">
       Outer planets
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Giant_planet" title="Giant planet">
       Giant planet
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gas_giants_in_the_solar_system.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2674" data-file-width="2075" height="284" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Gas_giants_in_the_solar_system.jpg/220px-Gas_giants_in_the_solar_system.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Gas_giants_in_the_solar_system.jpg/330px-Gas_giants_in_the_solar_system.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Gas_giants_in_the_solar_system.jpg/440px-Gas_giants_in_the_solar_system.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gas_giants_in_the_solar_system.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        From top to bottom:
        <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">
         Neptune
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">
         Uranus
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">
         Saturn
        </a>
        , and
        <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">
         Jupiter
        </a>
        (Montage with approximate colour and size)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The four outer planets, or giant planets (sometimes called Jovian planets), collectively make up 99% of the mass known to orbit the Sun.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-footnoteD_22-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-footnoteD-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        f
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Jupiter and Saturn are each many tens of times the mass of Earth and consist overwhelmingly of hydrogen and helium; Uranus and Neptune are far less massive (&lt;20 Earth masses) and possess more ices in their makeup. For these reasons, some astronomers suggest they belong in their own category, "ice giants".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
       <a href="#cite_note-95">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        89
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      All four giant planets have
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_ring" title="Planetary ring">
       rings
      </a>
      , although only Saturn's ring system is easily observed from Earth. The term
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superior_planet" title="Superior planet">
        superior planet
       </a>
      </i>
      designates planets outside Earth's orbit and thus includes both the outer planets and Mars.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Jupiter">
       Jupiter
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">
       Jupiter
      </a>
     </div>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">
        Jupiter
       </a>
       (5.2AU), at 318 Earth masses, is 2.5 times the mass of all the other planets put together. It is composed largely of
       <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">
        hydrogen
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">
        helium
       </a>
       . Jupiter's strong internal heat creates semi-permanent features in its atmosphere, such as cloud bands and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Red_Spot" title="Great Red Spot">
        Great Red Spot
       </a>
       . Jupiter has
       <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Jupiter" title="Moons of Jupiter">
        67 known satellites
       </a>
       . The four largest,
       <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">
        Ganymede
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">
        Callisto
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">
        Io
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">
        Europa
       </a>
       , show similarities to the terrestrial planets, such as volcanism and internal heating.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
        <a href="#cite_note-96">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         90
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       Ganymede, the largest satellite in the Solar System, is larger than Mercury.
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Saturn">
       Saturn
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">
       Saturn
      </a>
     </div>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">
        Saturn
       </a>
       (9.5AU), distinguished by its extensive
       <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn" title="Rings of Saturn">
        ring system
       </a>
       , has several similarities to Jupiter, such as its atmospheric composition and magnetosphere. Although Saturn has 60% of Jupiter's volume, it is less than a third as massive, at 95 Earth masses, making it the least dense planet in the Solar System.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-universetoday_97-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-universetoday-97">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         91
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       The rings of Saturn are made up of small ice and rock particles. Saturn has
       <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Saturn" title="Moons of Saturn">
        62 confirmed satellites
       </a>
       ; two of which,
       <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">
        Titan
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Enceladus" title="Enceladus">
        Enceladus
       </a>
       , show signs of geological activity, though they are largely
       <a href="/wiki/Cryovolcano" title="Cryovolcano">
        made of ice
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
        <a href="#cite_note-98">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         92
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       Titan, the second-largest moon in the Solar System, is larger than Mercury and the only satellite in the Solar System with a substantial atmosphere.
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Uranus">
       Uranus
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">
       Uranus
      </a>
     </div>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">
        Uranus
       </a>
       (19.2AU), at 14 Earth masses, is the lightest of the outer planets. Uniquely among the planets, it orbits the Sun on its side; its
       <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt" title="Axial tilt">
        axial tilt
       </a>
       is over ninety degrees to the
       <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">
        ecliptic
       </a>
       . It has a much colder core than the other giant planets and radiates very little heat into space.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
        <a href="#cite_note-99">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         93
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       Uranus has
       <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Uranus" title="Moons of Uranus">
        27 known satellites
       </a>
       , the largest ones being
       <a href="/wiki/Titania_(moon)" title="Titania (moon)">
        Titania
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Oberon_(moon)" title="Oberon (moon)">
        Oberon
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Umbriel_(moon)" title="Umbriel (moon)">
        Umbriel
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ariel_(moon)" title="Ariel (moon)">
        Ariel
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Miranda_(moon)" title="Miranda (moon)">
        Miranda
       </a>
       .
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Neptune">
       Neptune
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">
       Neptune
      </a>
     </div>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">
        Neptune
       </a>
       (30.1 AU), though slightly smaller than Uranus, is more massive (equivalent to 17 Earths) and hence more
       <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">
        dense
       </a>
       . It radiates more internal heat, but not as much as Jupiter or Saturn.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
        <a href="#cite_note-100">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         94
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       Neptune has
       <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Neptune" title="Moons of Neptune">
        14 known satellites
       </a>
       . The largest,
       <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">
        Triton
       </a>
       , is geologically active, with
       <a href="/wiki/Geyser" title="Geyser">
        geysers
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Liquid_nitrogen" title="Liquid nitrogen">
        liquid nitrogen
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
        <a href="#cite_note-101">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         95
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       Triton is the only large satellite with a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrograde_orbit" title="Retrograde orbit">
        retrograde orbit
       </a>
       . Neptune is accompanied in its orbit by several
       <a href="/wiki/Minor_planet" title="Minor planet">
        minor planets
       </a>
       , termed
       <a href="/wiki/Neptune_trojan" title="Neptune trojan">
        Neptune trojans
       </a>
       , that are in 1:1
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">
        resonance
       </a>
       with it.
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Centaurs">
       Centaurs
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Centaur_(minor_planet)" title="Centaur (minor planet)">
       Centaur (minor planet)
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The centaurs are icy comet-like bodies whose orbits have semi-major axes greater than Jupiter's (5.5AU) and less than Neptune's (30AU). The largest known centaur,
      <a href="/wiki/10199_Chariklo" title="10199 Chariklo">
       10199 Chariklo
      </a>
      , has a diameter of about 250km.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spitzer_102-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-spitzer-102">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        96
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The first centaur discovered,
      <a href="/wiki/2060_Chiron" title="2060 Chiron">
       2060 Chiron
      </a>
      , has also been classified as comet (95P) because it develops a coma just as comets do when they approach the Sun.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
       <a href="#cite_note-103">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        97
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Comets">
       Comets
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">
       Comet
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Comet_c1995o1.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="427" data-file-width="300" height="242" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Comet_c1995o1.jpg/170px-Comet_c1995o1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Comet_c1995o1.jpg/255px-Comet_c1995o1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/58/Comet_c1995o1.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Comet_c1995o1.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Comet_Hale%E2%80%93Bopp" title="Comet HaleBopp">
         Comet HaleBopp
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Comets are small Solar System bodies,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-footnoteB_13-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-footnoteB-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        d
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      typically only a few kilometres across, composed largely of volatile ices. They have highly eccentric orbits, generally a perihelion within the orbits of the inner planets and an aphelion far beyond Pluto. When a comet enters the inner Solar System, its proximity to the Sun causes its icy surface to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sublimation_(chemistry)" title="Sublimation (chemistry)">
       sublimate
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">
       ionise
      </a>
      , creating a
      <a href="/wiki/Coma_(cometary)" title="Coma (cometary)">
       coma
      </a>
      : a long tail of gas and dust often visible to the naked eye.
     </p>
     <p>
      Short-period comets have orbits lasting less than two hundred years. Long-period comets have orbits lasting thousands of years. Short-period comets are believed to originate in the Kuiper belt, whereas long-period comets, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Comet_Hale%E2%80%93Bopp" title="Comet HaleBopp">
       HaleBopp
      </a>
      , are believed to originate in the
      <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">
       Oort cloud
      </a>
      . Many comet groups, such as the
      <a href="/wiki/Kreutz_Sungrazers" title="Kreutz Sungrazers">
       Kreutz Sungrazers
      </a>
      , formed from the breakup of a single parent.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
       <a href="#cite_note-104">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        98
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some comets with
      <a href="/wiki/Hyperbolic_trajectory" title="Hyperbolic trajectory">
       hyperbolic
      </a>
      orbits may originate outside the Solar System, but determining their precise orbits is difficult.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hyperbolic_105-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-hyperbolic-105">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        99
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Old comets that have had most of their volatiles driven out by solar warming are often categorised as asteroids.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106">
       <a href="#cite_note-106">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        100
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Trans-Neptunian_region">
       Trans-Neptunian region
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The area beyond Neptune, or the "
      <a href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_object" title="Trans-Neptunian object">
       trans-Neptunian region
      </a>
      ", is still
      <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Solar_System_exploration" title="Timeline of Solar System exploration">
       largely unexplored
      </a>
      . It appears to consist overwhelmingly of small worlds (the largest having a diameter only a fifth that of Earth and a mass far smaller than that of the Moon) composed mainly of rock and ice. This region is sometimes known as the "outer Solar System", though others use that term to mean the region beyond the asteroid belt.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Kuiper_belt">
       Kuiper belt
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">
       Kuiper belt
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kuiper_belt_plot_objects_of_outer_solar_system.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1024" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Kuiper_belt_plot_objects_of_outer_solar_system.png/220px-Kuiper_belt_plot_objects_of_outer_solar_system.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Kuiper_belt_plot_objects_of_outer_solar_system.png/330px-Kuiper_belt_plot_objects_of_outer_solar_system.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Kuiper_belt_plot_objects_of_outer_solar_system.png/440px-Kuiper_belt_plot_objects_of_outer_solar_system.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kuiper_belt_plot_objects_of_outer_solar_system.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Known objects in the
        <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">
         Kuiper belt
        </a>
        as of January 2015.
        <table style="width:100%;">
         <tr>
          <td valign="top">
           <span style="margin:0px; font-size:90%;">
            <span style="border:1px solid #B3B300; background-color:#FFFF00; color:black;">
            </span>
            <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
             Sun
            </a>
           </span>
           <br/>
           <span style="margin:0px; font-size:90%;">
            <span style="border:1px solid #777; background-color:#aaa; color:black;">
            </span>
            <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_trojan" title="Jupiter trojan">
             Jupiter trojans
            </a>
           </span>
           <br/>
           <span style="margin:0px; font-size:90%;">
            <span style="border:1px solid #FF0000; background-color:#FF4D4D; color:black;">
            </span>
            <a href="/wiki/Giant_planet" title="Giant planet">
             Giant planets
            </a>
           </span>
          </td>
          <td valign="top">
           <span style="margin:0px; font-size:90%;">
            <span style="border:1px solid #00AAFF; background-color:#66CCFF; color:black;">
            </span>
            <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">
             Kuiper belt
            </a>
           </span>
           <br/>
           <span style="margin:0px; font-size:90%;">
            <span style="border:1px solid #FF950A; background-color:#FFB657; color:black;">
            </span>
            <a href="/wiki/Scattered_disc" title="Scattered disc">
             Scattered disc
            </a>
           </span>
           <br/>
           <span style="margin:0px; font-size:90%;">
            <span style="border:1px solid #550055; background-color:#A300A3; color:black;">
            </span>
            <a href="/wiki/Neptune_trojan" title="Neptune trojan">
             Neptune trojans
            </a>
           </span>
          </td>
         </tr>
        </table>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The Kuiper belt is a great ring of debris similar to the asteroid belt, but consisting mainly of objects composed primarily of ice.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-physical_107-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-physical-107">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        101
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It extends between 30 and 50AU from the Sun. Though it is estimated to contain anything from dozens to thousands of dwarf planets, it is composed mainly of small Solar System bodies. Many of the larger Kuiper belt objects, such as
      <a href="/wiki/50000_Quaoar" title="50000 Quaoar">
       Quaoar
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/20000_Varuna" title="20000 Varuna">
       Varuna
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/90482_Orcus" title="90482 Orcus">
       Orcus
      </a>
      , may prove to be dwarf planets with further data. There are estimated to be over 100,000 Kuiper belt objects with a diameter greater than 50km, but the total mass of the Kuiper belt is thought to be only a tenth or even a hundredth the mass of Earth.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Delsanti-Beyond_The_Planets_20-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Delsanti-Beyond_The_Planets-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Many Kuiper belt objects have multiple satellites,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">
       <a href="#cite_note-108">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        102
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and most have orbits that take them outside the plane of the ecliptic.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trojan_109-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-trojan-109">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        103
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Kuiper belt can be roughly divided into the "
      <a href="/wiki/Classical_Kuiper_belt_object" title="Classical Kuiper belt object">
       classical
      </a>
      " belt and the
      <a href="/wiki/Resonant_trans-Neptunian_object" title="Resonant trans-Neptunian object">
       resonances
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-physical_107-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-physical-107">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        101
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Resonances are orbits linked to that of Neptune (e.g. twice for every three Neptune orbits, or once for every two). The first resonance begins within the orbit of Neptune itself. The classical belt consists of objects having no resonance with Neptune, and extends from roughly 39.4AU to 47.7AU.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110">
       <a href="#cite_note-110">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        104
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Members of the classical Kuiper belt are classified as
      <a href="/wiki/Classical_Kuiper_belt_object" title="Classical Kuiper belt object">
       cubewanos
      </a>
      , after the first of their kind to be discovered,
      <span class="nowrap">
       <a href="/wiki/(15760)_1992_QB1" title="(15760) 1992 QB1">
        (15760) 1992 QB
        <span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;">
         <span style="font-size:80%;">
          1
         </span>
        </span>
       </a>
      </span>
      , and are still in near primordial, low-eccentricity orbits.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">
       <a href="#cite_note-111">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        105
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Pluto_and_Charon">
       Pluto and Charon
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;">
       <div class="noresize">
        <map id="ImageMap_1_120284900" name="ImageMap_1_120284900">

         </area>
         <area alt="Eris" coords="39,71,22" href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" shape="circle" title="Eris">
         </area>
         <area alt="Charon" coords="141,77,10" href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" shape="circle" title="Charon">

         </area>
         <area alt="Haumea" coords="275,69,17" href="/wiki/Haumea" shape="circle" title="Haumea">
         </area>
         <area alt="Sedna" coords="39,147,15" href="/wiki/90377_Sedna" shape="circle" title="Sedna">

         </area>
        </map>
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1995" data-file-width="2750" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/EightTNOs.png/310px-EightTNOs.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/EightTNOs.png/465px-EightTNOs.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/EightTNOs.png/620px-EightTNOs.png 2x" usemap="#ImageMap_1_120284900" width="310"/>
       </div>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EightTNOs.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Artistic comparison of
        <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">

        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">
         Pluto
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Makemake" title="Makemake">
         Makemake
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Haumea" title="Haumea">
         Haumea
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/90377_Sedna" title="90377 Sedna">
         Sedna
        </a>
        ,
        <span class="nowrap">

          <sub>
           10
          </sub>
         </a>
        </span>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/50000_Quaoar" title="50000 Quaoar">
         Quaoar
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/90482_Orcus" title="90482 Orcus">
         Orcus
        </a>
        , and
        <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">
         Earth
        </a>
        .
        <br/>
        (
        <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar">
         <span style="word-spacing:0">

         </span>
         <ul>
          <li class="nv-view">
           <a href="/wiki/Template:TNO_imagemap" title="Template:TNO imagemap">

          <li class="nv-talk">
