             talk
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
          <li class="nv-edit">
           <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:TNO_imagemap&amp;action=edit">
            <span title="Edit this template">
             edit
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
         </ul>
        </div>
        )
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pluto_(dwarf_planet)" title="Pluto (dwarf planet)">
       Pluto (dwarf planet)
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">
       Charon (moon)
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The dwarf planet
      <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">
       Pluto
      </a>
      (39AU average) is the largest known object in the Kuiper belt. When discovered in 1930, it was considered to be the ninth planet; this changed in 2006 with the adoption of a formal
      <a href="/wiki/Definition_of_planet" title="Definition of planet">
       definition of planet
      </a>
      . Pluto has a relatively eccentric orbit inclined 17 degrees to the ecliptic plane and ranging from 29.7AU from the Sun at perihelion (within the orbit of Neptune) to 49.5AU at aphelion.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">
       Charon
      </a>
      , Pluto's largest moon, is sometimes described as part of a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Binary_system_(astronomy)" title="Binary system (astronomy)">
       binary system
      </a>
      with Pluto, as the two bodies orbit a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth-Moon_barycenter" title="Earth-Moon barycenter">
       barycentre
      </a>
      of gravity above their surfaces (i.e. they appear to "orbit each other"). Beyond Charon, four much smaller moons,
      <a href="/wiki/Styx_(moon)" title="Styx (moon)">
       Styx
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Nix_(moon)" title="Nix (moon)">
       Nix
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Kerberos_(moon)" title="Kerberos (moon)">
       Kerberos
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Hydra_(moon)" title="Hydra (moon)">
       Hydra
      </a>
      , are known to orbit within the system.
     </p>
     <p>
      Pluto has a 3:2
      <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">
       resonance
      </a>
      with Neptune, meaning that Pluto orbits twice round the Sun for every three Neptunian orbits. Kuiper belt objects whose orbits share this resonance are called
      <a href="/wiki/Plutino" title="Plutino">
       plutinos
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fajans_et_al_2001_112-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Fajans_et_al_2001-112">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        106
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Makemake_and_Haumea">
       Makemake and Haumea
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Makemake" title="Makemake">
       Makemake
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Haumea" title="Haumea">
       Haumea
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Makemake" title="Makemake">
       Makemake
      </a>
      (45.79AU average), although smaller than Pluto, is the largest known object in the
      <a href="/wiki/Classical_Kuiper_belt_object" title="Classical Kuiper belt object">
       <i>
        classical
       </i>
       Kuiper belt
      </a>
      (that is, a Kuiper belt object not in a confirmed
      <a href="/wiki/Resonant_trans-Neptunian_object" title="Resonant trans-Neptunian object">
       resonance
      </a>
      with Neptune). Makemake is the brightest object in the Kuiper belt after Pluto. It was named and designated a dwarf planet in 2008.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-name_10-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-name-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Its orbit is far more inclined than Pluto's, at 29.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buie136472_113-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Buie136472-113">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        107
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Haumea" title="Haumea">
       Haumea
      </a>
      (43.13AU average) is in an orbit similar to Makemake except that it is in a 7:12 orbital resonance with Neptune.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brownlargest_114-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-brownlargest-114">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        108
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is about the same size as Makemake and has two natural satellites. A rapid, 3.9-hour rotation gives it a flattened and elongated shape. It was named and designated a dwarf planet in 2008.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iaunews_115-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-iaunews-115">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        109
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Scattered_disc">
       Scattered disc
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Scattered_disc" title="Scattered disc">
       Scattered disc
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The scattered disc, which overlaps the Kuiper belt but extends much further outwards, is thought to be the source of short-period comets. Scattered disc objects are believed to have been ejected into erratic orbits by the gravitational influence of
      <a href="/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System#Planetary_migration" title="Formation and evolution of the Solar System">
       Neptune's early outward migration
      </a>
      . Most scattered disc objects (SDOs) have perihelia within the Kuiper belt but aphelia far beyond it (some more than 150AU from the Sun). SDOs' orbits are also highly inclined to the ecliptic plane and are often almost perpendicular to it. Some astronomers consider the scattered disc to be merely another region of the Kuiper belt and describe scattered disc objects as "scattered Kuiper belt objects".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116">
       <a href="#cite_note-116">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        110
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some astronomers also classify centaurs as inward-scattered Kuiper belt objects along with the outward-scattered residents of the scattered disc.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">
       <a href="#cite_note-117">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        111
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Eris">
       Eris
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">
       Eris (dwarf planet)
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">
       Eris
      </a>
      (68AU average) is the largest known scattered disc object, and caused a debate about what constitutes a planet, because it is 25% more massive than Pluto
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_Schaller_2007_118-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brown_Schaller_2007-118">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        112
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and about the same diameter. It is the most massive of the known dwarf planets. It has one known moon,
      <a href="/wiki/Dysnomia_(moon)" title="Dysnomia (moon)">
       Dysnomia
      </a>
      . Like Pluto, its orbit is highly eccentric, with a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion" title="Perihelion">
       perihelion
      </a>
      of 38.2 AU (roughly Pluto's distance from the Sun) and an
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aphelion" title="Aphelion">
       aphelion
      </a>
      of 97.6 AU, and steeply inclined to the ecliptic plane.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Farthest_regions">
       Farthest regions
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The point at which the Solar System ends and interstellar space begins is not precisely defined because its outer boundaries are shaped by two separate forces: the solar wind and the Sun's gravity. The outer limit of the solar wind's influence is roughly four times Pluto's distance from the Sun; this
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heliopause_(astronomy)" title="Heliopause (astronomy)">
        heliopause
       </a>
      </i>
      is considered the beginning of the
      <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">
       interstellar medium
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Voyager_62-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Voyager-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Sun's
      <a href="/wiki/Hill_sphere" title="Hill sphere">
       Hill sphere
      </a>
      , the effective range of its gravitational dominance, is believed to extend up to a thousand times farther.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Littmann_119-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Littmann-119">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        113
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Heliopause">
       Heliopause
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:IBEX_all_sky_map.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="180" data-file-width="320" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/IBEX_all_sky_map.jpg/220px-IBEX_all_sky_map.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ec/IBEX_all_sky_map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ec/IBEX_all_sky_map.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:IBEX_all_sky_map.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energetic_neutral_atoms" title="Energetic neutral atoms">
         Energetic neutral atoms
        </a>
        map of heliosheath and heliopause by
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IBEX" title="IBEX">
         IBEX
        </a>
        . Credit: NASA/Goddard Space Flight Center Scientific Visualization Studio.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The heliosphere is divided into two regions; the solar wind travels at roughly 400km/s until it collides with the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstellar_wind" title="Interstellar wind">
       interstellar wind
      </a>
      ; the flow of plasma in the
      <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">
       interstellar medium
      </a>
      . The collision occurs at the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Termination_shock" title="Termination shock">
       termination shock
      </a>
      , which is roughly 80100 AU from the Sun upwind of the interstellar medium and roughly 200 AU from the Sun downwind.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fahr_120-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-fahr-120">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        114
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Here the wind slows dramatically, condenses and becomes more turbulent,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fahr_120-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-fahr-120">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        114
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      forming a great oval structure known as the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heliosheath" title="Heliosheath">
       heliosheath
      </a>
      . This structure is believed to look and behave very much like a comet's tail, extending outward for a further 40 AU on the upwind side but tailing many times that distance downwind; evidence from the Cassini and
      <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_Boundary_Explorer" title="Interstellar Boundary Explorer">
       Interstellar Boundary Explorer
      </a>
      spacecraft has suggested that it is forced into a bubble shape by the constraining action of the interstellar magnetic field.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121">
       <a href="#cite_note-121">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        115
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The outer boundary of the heliosphere, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heliopause_(astronomy)" title="Heliopause (astronomy)">
       heliopause
      </a>
      , is the point at which the solar wind finally terminates and is the beginning of interstellar space.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Voyager_62-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Voyager-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">
        Voyager 1
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">
        Voyager 2
       </a>
      </i>
      are reported to have passed the termination shock and entered the heliosheath, at 94 and 84 AU from the Sun, respectively.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122">
       <a href="#cite_note-122">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        116
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123">
       <a href="#cite_note-123">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        117
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <i>
       Voyager 1
      </i>
      is reported to have crossed the heliopause in August, 2012.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20130912_124-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NASA-20130912-124">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        118
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The shape and form of the outer edge of the heliosphere is likely affected by the
      <a href="/wiki/Fluid_dynamics" title="Fluid dynamics">
       fluid dynamics
      </a>
      of interactions with the interstellar medium as well as solar magnetic fields prevailing to the south, e.g. it is bluntly shaped with the northern hemisphere extending 9 AU farther than the southern hemisphere.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fahr_120-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-fahr-120">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        114
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Beyond the heliopause, at around 230 AU, lies the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bow_shock" title="Bow shock">
       bow shock
      </a>
      , a plasma "wake" left by the Sun as it travels through the
      <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">
       Milky Way
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125">
       <a href="#cite_note-125">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        119
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Due to a lack of data, conditions in local interstellar space are not known for certain. It is expected that
      <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">
       NASA
      </a>
      's
      <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">
       <i>
        Voyager
       </i>
       spacecraft
      </a>
      , as they pass the heliopause, will transmit valuable data on radiation levels and solar wind to Earth.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126">
       <a href="#cite_note-126">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        120
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      How well the heliosphere shields the Solar System from cosmic rays is poorly understood. A NASA-funded team has developed a concept of a "Vision Mission" dedicated to sending a probe to the heliosphere.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127">
       <a href="#cite_note-127">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        121
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128">
       <a href="#cite_note-128">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        122
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Detached_objects">
       Detached objects
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Detached_object" title="Detached object">
       Detached object
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Sednoid" title="Sednoid">
       Sednoid
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/90377_Sedna" title="90377 Sedna">
       90377 Sedna
      </a>
      (520 AU average) is a large, reddish object with a gigantic, highly elliptical orbit that takes it from about 76AU at perihelion to 940AU at aphelion and takes 11,400 years to complete.
      <a href="/wiki/Michael_E._Brown" title="Michael E. Brown">
       Mike Brown
      </a>
      , who discovered the object in 2003, asserts that it cannot be part of the
      <a href="/wiki/Scattered_disc" title="Scattered disc">
       scattered disc
      </a>
      or the Kuiper belt because its perihelion is too distant to have been affected by Neptune's migration. He and other astronomers consider it to be the first in an entirely new population, sometimes termed "distant detached objects" (DDOs), which also may include the object
      <span class="nowrap">
       <a href="/wiki/(148209)_2000_CR105" title="(148209) 2000 CR105">
        2000 CR
        <sub>
         105
        </sub>
       </a>
      </span>
      , which has a perihelion of 45AU, an aphelion of 415AU, and an orbital period of 3,420 years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129">
       <a href="#cite_note-129">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        123
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Brown terms this population the "inner Oort cloud" because it may have formed through a similar process, although it is far closer to the Sun.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130">
       <a href="#cite_note-130">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        124
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Sedna is very likely a dwarf planet, though its shape has yet to be determined. The second unequivocally detached object, with a perihelion farther than Sedna's at roughly 81 AU, is
      <span class="nowrap">
       <a href="/wiki/2012_VP113" title="2012 VP113">
        2012 VP
        <span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;">
         <span style="font-size:80%;">
          113
         </span>
        </span>
       </a>
      </span>
      , discovered in 2012. Its aphelion is only half that of Sedna's, at 400500 AU.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpldata_2012_VP113_131-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-jpldata_2012_VP113-131">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        125
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Physorg_132-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Physorg-132">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        126
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Oort_cloud">
       Oort cloud
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">
       Oort cloud
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kuiper_belt_-_Oort_cloud-en.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="439" data-file-width="512" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Kuiper_belt_-_Oort_cloud-en.svg/290px-Kuiper_belt_-_Oort_cloud-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Kuiper_belt_-_Oort_cloud-en.svg/435px-Kuiper_belt_-_Oort_cloud-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Kuiper_belt_-_Oort_cloud-en.svg/580px-Kuiper_belt_-_Oort_cloud-en.svg.png 2x" width="290"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kuiper_belt_-_Oort_cloud-en.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        An artist's rendering of the Oort cloud, the Hills cloud, and the Kuiper belt (inset)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The Oort cloud is a hypothetical spherical cloud of up to a trillion icy objects that is believed to be the source for all long-period comets and to surround the Solar System at roughly 50,000AU (around 1
      <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">
       light-year
      </a>
      (ly)), and possibly to as far as 100,000AU (1.87ly). It is believed to be composed of comets that were ejected from the inner Solar System by gravitational interactions with the outer planets. Oort cloud objects move very slowly, and can be perturbed by infrequent events, such as collisions, the gravitational effects of a passing star, or the
      <a href="/wiki/Galactic_tide" title="Galactic tide">
       galactic tide
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Tidal_force" title="Tidal force">
       tidal force
      </a>
      exerted by the
      <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">
       Milky Way
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133">
       <a href="#cite_note-133">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        127
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134">
       <a href="#cite_note-134">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        128
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Boundaries">
       Boundaries
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Vulcanoid_asteroid" title="Vulcanoid asteroid">
       Vulcanoid asteroid
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Planets_beyond_Neptune" title="Planets beyond Neptune">
       Planets beyond Neptune
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Much of the Solar System is still unknown. The Sun's gravitational field is estimated to dominate the gravitational forces of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_nearest_stars" title="List of nearest stars">
       surrounding stars
      </a>
      out to about two light years (125,000 AU). Lower estimates for the radius of the Oort cloud, by contrast, do not place it farther than 50,000 AU.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135">
       <a href="#cite_note-135">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        129
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Despite discoveries such as Sedna, the region between the Kuiper belt and the Oort cloud, an area tens of thousands of AU in radius, is still virtually unmapped. There are also ongoing studies of the region between Mercury and the Sun.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136">
       <a href="#cite_note-136">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        130
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Objects may yet be discovered in the Solar System's uncharted regions.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Galactic_context">
       Galactic context
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <div class="thumbimage" style="width:300px;">
        <div style="position: relative; width: 300px;">
         <div style="position: absolute; left: 142px; top: 55px; padding: 0;">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="1045" data-file-width="518" height="32" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Yellow_Arrow_Down.png/16px-Yellow_Arrow_Down.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Yellow_Arrow_Down.png/24px-Yellow_Arrow_Down.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Yellow_Arrow_Down.png/32px-Yellow_Arrow_Down.png 2x" width="16"/>
         </div>
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Milky_Way_Arms_ssc2008-10.svg" title="Position of the Solar System within the Milky Way">
          <img alt="Position of the Solar System within the Milky Way" data-file-height="461" data-file-width="512" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Milky_Way_Arms_ssc2008-10.svg/300px-Milky_Way_Arms_ssc2008-10.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Milky_Way_Arms_ssc2008-10.svg/450px-Milky_Way_Arms_ssc2008-10.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Milky_Way_Arms_ssc2008-10.svg/600px-Milky_Way_Arms_ssc2008-10.svg.png 2x" width="300"/>
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        Position of the Solar System within the Milky Way
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The Solar System is located in the
      <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">
       Milky Way
      </a>
      , a
      <a href="/wiki/Barred_spiral_galaxy" title="Barred spiral galaxy">
       barred spiral galaxy
      </a>
      with a diameter of about 100,000
      <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">
       light-years
      </a>
      containing about 200 billion stars.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fn9_137-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-fn9-137">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        131
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Sun resides in one of the Milky Way's outer spiral arms, known as the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orion%E2%80%93Cygnus_Arm" title="OrionCygnus Arm">
       OrionCygnus Arm
      </a>
      or Local Spur.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138">
       <a href="#cite_note-138">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        132
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Sun lies between 25,000 and 28,000 light years from the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galactic_Centre" title="Galactic Centre">
       Galactic Centre
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-distance2_139-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-distance2-139">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        133
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and its speed within the Milky Way is about 220
      <a href="/wiki/Metre_per_second" title="Metre per second">
       kilometres per second
      </a>
      (140mi/s), so that it completes one revolution every 225250 million years. This revolution is known as the Solar System's
      <a href="/wiki/Galactic_year" title="Galactic year">
       galactic year
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140">
       <a href="#cite_note-140">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        134
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_apex" title="Solar apex">
       solar apex
      </a>
      , the direction of the Sun's path through interstellar space, is near the constellation
      <a href="/wiki/Hercules_(constellation)" title="Hercules (constellation)">
       Hercules
      </a>
      in the direction of the current location of the bright star
      <a href="/wiki/Vega" title="Vega">
       Vega
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141">
       <a href="#cite_note-141">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        135
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The plane of the ecliptic lies at an angle of about 60 to the
      <a href="/wiki/Galactic_plane" title="Galactic plane">
       galactic plane
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143">
       <a href="#cite_note-143">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        g
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Solar System's location in the Milky Way is a factor in the
      <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">
       evolution
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">
       life
      </a>
      on Earth. Its orbit is close to circular, and orbits near the Sun are at roughly the same speed as that of the spiral arms.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astrobiology_144-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-astrobiology-144">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        137
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145">
       <a href="#cite_note-145">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        138
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Therefore, the Sun passes through arms only rarely. Because spiral arms are home to a far larger concentration of
      <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">
       supernovae
      </a>
      , gravitational instabilities, and radiation that could disrupt the Solar System, this has given Earth long periods of stability for life to evolve.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astrobiology_144-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-astrobiology-144">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        137
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Solar System also lies well outside the star-crowded environs of the galactic centre. Near the centre, gravitational tugs from nearby stars could perturb bodies in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oort_Cloud" title="Oort Cloud">
       Oort Cloud
      </a>
      and send many comets into the inner Solar System, producing collisions with potentially catastrophic implications for life on
      <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">
       Earth
      </a>
      . The intense radiation of the galactic centre could also interfere with the development of complex life.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astrobiology_144-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-astrobiology-144">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        137
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Even at the Solar System's current location, some scientists have speculated that recent
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supernovae" title="Supernovae">
       supernovae
      </a>
      may have adversely affected life in the last 35,000 years, by flinging pieces of expelled stellar core towards the Sun, as radioactive dust grains and larger, comet-like bodies.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146">
       <a href="#cite_note-146">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        139
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Neighbourhood">
       Neighbourhood
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Local_Interstellar_Clouds_with_motion_arrows.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="4000" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Local_Interstellar_Clouds_with_motion_arrows.jpg/220px-Local_Interstellar_Clouds_with_motion_arrows.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Local_Interstellar_Clouds_with_motion_arrows.jpg/330px-Local_Interstellar_Clouds_with_motion_arrows.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Local_Interstellar_Clouds_with_motion_arrows.jpg/440px-Local_Interstellar_Clouds_with_motion_arrows.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Local_Interstellar_Clouds_with_motion_arrows.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Beyond the heliosphere is the interstellar medium, consisting of various clouds of gases. (see
        <a href="/wiki/Local_Interstellar_Cloud" title="Local Interstellar Cloud">
         Local Interstellar Cloud
        </a>
        )
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The Solar System is in the
      <a href="/wiki/Local_Interstellar_Cloud" title="Local Interstellar Cloud">
       Local Interstellar Cloud
      </a>
      or Local Fluff. It is thought to be near the neighbouring
      <a href="/wiki/G-Cloud" title="G-Cloud">
       G-Cloud
      </a>
      but it is not known if the Solar System is embedded in the Local Interstellar Cloud, or if it is in the region where the Local Interstellar Cloud and G-Cloud are interacting.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147">
       <a href="#cite_note-147">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        140
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148">
       <a href="#cite_note-148">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        141
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Local Interstellar Cloud is an area of denser cloud in an otherwise sparse region known as the
      <a href="/wiki/Local_Bubble" title="Local Bubble">
       Local Bubble
      </a>
      , an hourglass-shaped cavity in the
      <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">
       interstellar medium
      </a>
      roughly 300 light years across. The bubble is suffused with high-temperature plasma, that suggests it is the product of several recent supernovae.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149">
       <a href="#cite_note-149">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        142
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      There are relatively few
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_nearest_stars" title="List of nearest stars">
       stars
      </a>
      within ten light years (95 trillion km, or 60 trillion mi) of the Sun. The closest is the triple star system
      <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Centauri" title="Alpha Centauri">
       Alpha Centauri
      </a>
      , which is about 4.4 light years away. Alpha Centauri A and B are a closely tied pair of Sun-like stars, whereas the small
      <a href="/wiki/Red_dwarf" title="Red dwarf">
       red dwarf
      </a>
      Alpha Centauri C (also known as
      <a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri" title="Proxima Centauri">
       Proxima Centauri
      </a>
      ) orbits the pair at a distance of 0.2 light years. The stars next closest to the Sun are the red dwarfs
      <a href="/wiki/Barnard%27s_Star" title="Barnard's Star">
       Barnard's Star
      </a>
      (at 5.9 light years),
      <a href="/wiki/Wolf_359" title="Wolf 359">
       Wolf 359
      </a>
      (7.8 light years), and
      <a href="/wiki/Lalande_21185" title="Lalande 21185">
       Lalande 21185
      </a>
      (8.3 light years). The largest star within ten light years is
      <a href="/wiki/Sirius" title="Sirius">
       Sirius
      </a>
      , a bright
      <a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">
       main-sequence
      </a>
      star roughly 8.6 light years away and roughly twice the Sun's mass and that is orbited by a
      <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">
       white dwarf
      </a>
      , Sirius B. The nearest
      <a href="/wiki/Brown_dwarf" title="Brown dwarf">
       brown dwarfs
      </a>
      are the binary
      <a href="/wiki/Luhman_16" title="Luhman 16">
       Luhman 16
      </a>
      system at 6.6 light years. The remaining systems within ten light years are the binary red-dwarf system
      <a href="/wiki/Luyten_726-8" title="Luyten 726-8">
       Luyten 726-8
      </a>
      (8.7 light years) and the solitary red dwarf
      <a href="/wiki/Ross_154" title="Ross 154">
       Ross 154
      </a>
      (9.7 light years).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150">
       <a href="#cite_note-150">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        143
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The closest solitary Sun-like star to the Solar System is
      <a href="/wiki/Tau_Ceti" title="Tau Ceti">
       Tau Ceti
      </a>
      at 11.9 light years. It has roughly 80% of the Sun's mass but only 60% of its luminosity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151">
       <a href="#cite_note-151">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        144
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The closest known
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extrasolar_planet" title="Extrasolar planet">
       extrasolar planet
      </a>
      to the Sun orbits Alpha Centauri B. This planet,
      <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Centauri_Bb" title="Alpha Centauri Bb">
       Alpha Centauri Bb
      </a>
      , is at least 1.1 times Earth's mass and orbits its star every 3.236 days.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152">
       <a href="#cite_note-152">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        145
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The closest known
      <a href="/wiki/Rogue_planet" title="Rogue planet">
       free-floating
      </a>
      planetary-mass object to the Sun is
      <a href="/wiki/WISE_0855%E2%88%920714" title="WISE 08550714">
       WISE 08550714
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153">
       <a href="#cite_note-153">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        146
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      an object with a mass less than 10
      <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_mass" title="Jupiter mass">
       Jupiter masses
      </a>
      located roughly 7 light years away.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; width:99%; max-width:2008px;">
      <div class="thumbinner">
       <div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Earth%27s_Location_in_the_Universe_(JPEG).jpg" title="A diagram of Earth's location in the observable Universe. (Click here for an alternate image.)">
         <img alt="" data-file-height="2953" data-file-width="23624" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Earth%27s_Location_in_the_Universe_%28JPEG%29.jpg/2000px-Earth%27s_Location_in_the_Universe_%28JPEG%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Earth%27s_Location_in_the_Universe_%28JPEG%29.jpg/3000px-Earth%27s_Location_in_the_Universe_%28JPEG%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Earth%27s_Location_in_the_Universe_%28JPEG%29.jpg/4000px-Earth%27s_Location_in_the_Universe_%28JPEG%29.jpg 2x" width="2000"/>
        </a>
       </div>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Earth%27s_Location_in_the_Universe_(JPEG).jpg" title="File:Earth's Location in the Universe (JPEG).jpg">
         </a>
        </div>
        A diagram of Earth's location in the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Observable_Universe" title="Observable Universe">
         observable Universe
        </a>
        . (
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/File:Earth%27s_Location_in_the_Universe_SMALLER_(JPEG).jpg" title="File:Earth's Location in the Universe SMALLER (JPEG).jpg">
          Click here for an alternate image
         </a>
         .
        </i>
        )
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Visual_summary">
       Visual summary
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>

      <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">
       Eris
      </a>
      , because these have not been imaged in high quality.
     </p>

        </a>
        <br/>
        <small>
         (moon of Saturn)
        </small>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr style="background: black;">
       <td>
        <div class="center">
         <div class="floatnone">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA09813_Epimetheus_S._polar_region.jpg">
           <img alt="PIA09813 Epimetheus S. polar region.jpg" data-file-height="580" data-file-width="600" height="97" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/PIA09813_Epimetheus_S._polar_region.jpg/100px-PIA09813_Epimetheus_S._polar_region.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/PIA09813_Epimetheus_S._polar_region.jpg/150px-PIA09813_Epimetheus_S._polar_region.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/PIA09813_Epimetheus_S._polar_region.jpg/200px-PIA09813_Epimetheus_S._polar_region.jpg 2x" width="100"/>
          </a>
         </div>
        </div>
       </td>
       <td>
        <div class="center">
         <div class="floatnone">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prometheus_12-26-09a.jpg">
           <img alt="Prometheus 12-26-09a.jpg" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="700" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Prometheus_12-26-09a.jpg/100px-Prometheus_12-26-09a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Prometheus_12-26-09a.jpg/150px-Prometheus_12-26-09a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Prometheus_12-26-09a.jpg/200px-Prometheus_12-26-09a.jpg 2x" width="100"/>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">
       Notes
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
      <ol class="references">
       <li id="cite_note-6">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-6">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <a href="/wiki/Capitalization" title="Capitalization">
          Capitalization
         </a>
         of the name varies. The
         <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">
          IAU
         </a>
         , the authoritative body regarding astronomical nomenclature, specifies capitalizing the names of all individual astronomical objects, but uses mixed "Solar System" and "solar system" in their
         <a class="external text" href="http://www.iau.org/public/themes/naming/" rel="nofollow">
          naming guidelines document
         </a>
         . The name is commonly rendered in lower case ("
         <b>
          solar system
         </b>
         "), as, for example, in the
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">
           Oxford English Dictionary
          </a>
         </i>
         and
         <a class="external text" href="http://www.m-w.com/dictionary/solar%20system" rel="nofollow">
          <i>
           Merriam-Webster's 11th Collegiate Dictionary
          </i>
         </a>
         .
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-7">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-7">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         The
         <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">
          moons
         </a>
         orbiting
         <a href="/wiki/List_of_gravitationally_rounded_objects_of_the_Solar_System#Planets" title="List of gravitationally rounded objects of the Solar System">
          the Solar System's planets
         </a>
         are an example of the latter.
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-8">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-8">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Historically, several other bodies were once considered planets, including, from its discovery in 1930 until 2006,
         <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">
          Pluto
         </a>
         . See
         <a href="/wiki/Planet#Former_planets" title="Planet">
          Former planets
         </a>
         .
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-footnoteB-13">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         ^
         <a href="#cite_ref-footnoteB_13-0">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             a
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
         <a href="#cite_ref-footnoteB_13-1">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             b
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
         <a href="#cite_ref-footnoteB_13-2">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             c
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
         <a href="#cite_ref-footnoteB_13-3">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             d
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
         <a href="#cite_ref-footnoteB_13-4">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             e
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
         <a href="#cite_ref-footnoteB_13-5">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             f
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         According to
         <a href="/wiki/IAU_definition_of_planet" title="IAU definition of planet">
          current definitions
         </a>
         , objects in orbit around the Sun are classified dynamically and physically into three categories:
         <i>
          planets
         </i>
         ,
         <i>
          dwarf planets
         </i>
         , and
         <i>
          small Solar System bodies
         </i>
         .
         <br/>
         <br/>
         A
         <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">
          planet
         </a>
         is any body in orbit around the Sun whose
         <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">
          mass
         </a>
         is sufficient for
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitation" title="Gravitation">
          gravity
         </a>
         to have pulled it into a (near-)
         <a href="/wiki/Sphere" title="Sphere">
          spherical
         </a>
         shape and which has
         <a href="/wiki/Clearing_the_neighbourhood" title="Clearing the neighbourhood">
          cleared its immediate neighbourhood
         </a>
         of all smaller objects. By this definition, the Solar System has eight planets: Mercury, Venus, Earth, Mars, Jupiter, Saturn, Uranus, and Neptune. As it has not cleared its neighbourhood of other
         <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">
          Kuiper belt
         </a>
         objects, Pluto does not fit this definition.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FinalResolution_9-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-FinalResolution-9">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           6
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
         <br/>
         <br/>
         Instead, Pluto is considered to be a
         <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">
          dwarf planet
         </a>
         , a body orbiting the Sun that is massive enough to be made near-spherical by its own gravity but which has not cleared
         <a href="/wiki/Planetesimal" title="Planetesimal">
          planetesimals
         </a>
         from its neighbourhood and is also not a satellite.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FinalResolution_9-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-FinalResolution-9">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           6
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
         In addition to Pluto, the
         <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">
          IAU
         </a>
         has recognized four other dwarf planets in the Solar System: Ceres, Haumea, Makemake, and Eris.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-name_10-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-name-10">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           7
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
         Other objects commonly (but not officially) treated as dwarf planets include
         <span class="nowrap">
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2007_OR10" title="2007 OR10">
           2007 OR
           <span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;">
            <span style="font-size:80%;">
             10
            </span>
           </span>
          </a>
         </span>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/90377_Sedna" title="90377 Sedna">
          Sedna
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/90482_Orcus" title="90482 Orcus">
          Orcus
         </a>
         , and
         <a href="/wiki/50000_Quaoar" title="50000 Quaoar">
          Quaoar
         </a>
         .
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
          <a href="#cite_note-11">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           8
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
         In a reference to Pluto, other dwarf planets orbiting in the
         <a href="#Trans-Neptunian_region">
          trans-Neptunian region
         </a>
         are sometimes called "
         <a href="/wiki/Plutoid" title="Plutoid">
          plutoids
         </a>
         ".
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU0804_12-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-IAU0804-12">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           9
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
         <br/>
         <br/>
         The remaining objects in orbit around the Sun are known as
         <a href="/wiki/Small_Solar_System_body" title="Small Solar System body">
          small Solar System bodies
         </a>
         .
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FinalResolution_9-2">
          <a href="#cite_note-FinalResolution-9">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           6
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-15">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-15">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         See
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_natural_satellites_of_the_Solar_System" title="List of natural satellites of the Solar System">
          List of natural satellites of the Solar System
         </a>
         for the full list of natural satellites of the eight planets and first five dwarf planets.
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-footnoteD-22">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         ^
         <a href="#cite_ref-footnoteD_22-0">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             a
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
         <a href="#cite_ref-footnoteD_22-1">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             b
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         The mass of the Solar System excluding the Sun, Jupiter and Saturn can be determined by adding together all the calculated masses for its largest objects and using rough calculations for the masses of the Oort cloud (estimated at roughly 3 Earth masses),
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
          <a href="#cite_note-19">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           14
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
         the Kuiper belt (estimated at roughly 0.1 Earth mass)
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Delsanti-Beyond_The_Planets_20-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-Delsanti-Beyond_The_Planets-20">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           15
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
         and the asteroid belt (estimated to be 0.0005 Earth mass)
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krasinsky2002_21-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-Krasinsky2002-21">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           16
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
         for a total, rounded upwards, of ~37 Earth masses, or 8.1% of the mass in orbit around the Sun. With the combined masses of Uranus and Neptune (~31 Earth masses) subtracted, the remaining ~6 Earth masses of material comprise 1.3% of the total orbiting mass.
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-143">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-143">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         If  is the angle between the
         <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic_pole" title="Ecliptic pole">
          north pole of the ecliptic
         </a>
         and the north
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galactic_pole" title="Galactic pole">
          galactic pole
         </a>
         then:
        </span>
        <dl>
         <dd>
          <span class="reference-text">
           <img alt="\cos\psi=\cos(\beta_g)\cos(\beta_e)\cos(\alpha_g-\alpha_e)+\sin(\beta_g)\sin(\beta_e)" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/3/2/3/323b6d72f3939f2bb7c20d1f6f234e06.png"/>
           ,
          </span>
         </dd>
        </dl>
        <span class="reference-text">
         where
         <img alt="\beta_g=" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/4/6/7/467b758b1a4ad6de6bddaee9852f9667.png"/>
         27 07 42.01 and
         <img alt="\alpha_g=" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/d/a/8/da89e72cc4f471fd928672cbdc52b394.png"/>
         12h 51m 26.282 are the declination and right ascension of the north galactic pole,
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142">
          <a href="#cite_note-142">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           136
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
         whereas
         <img alt="\beta_e=" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/3/2/b/32bc971013ba11fa9f19c74a007939bb.png"/>
         66 33 38.6 and
         <img alt="\alpha_e=" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/a/d/9/ad9c709254cbd5688d54efb398c939f8.png"/>
         18h 0m 00 are those for the north pole of the ecliptic. (Both pairs of coordinates are for
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J2000" title="J2000">
          J2000
         </a>
         epoch.) The result of the calculation is 60.19.
        </span>
       </li>
      </ol>
     </div>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="References">
       References
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
      <ol class="references">

       <li id="cite_note-43">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-43">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         The date is based on the oldest
         <a href="/wiki/Inclusion_(mineral)" title="Inclusion (mineral)">
          inclusions
         </a>
         found to date in
         <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">
          meteorites
         </a>
         , and is thought to be the date of the formation of the first solid material in the collapsing nebula.
         <br/>
         A. Bouvier and M. Wadhwa. "The age of the solar system redefined by the oldest Pb-Pb age of a meteoritic inclusion."
         <i>
          Nature Geoscience,
         </i>
         in press, 2010.
         <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">
          doi
         </a>
         :
         <a class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1038%2FNGEO941" rel="nofollow">
          10.1038/NGEO941
         </a>
        </span>

         </a>
        </span>
       </li>
